import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels=3, out_channels=96, kernel_size=11, stride=4, padding=2)
    
    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv1(x)
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 256
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, 3, 224, 224)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

The reference is a simple conv2d with these parameters. We need to write a fused CUDA kernel that performs this convolution faster than PyTorch's cuDNN or default conv implementation. Since input channels=3 (small), this is an RGB image conv. Could be memory-bound. We need to write an efficient kernel. We can consider using im2col + GEMM, but writing custom GEMM is heavy. Alternatively, we can write a direct convolution kernel that fuses loading of input and weights, exploiting the small number of input channels and specific kernel size. Better: Use block tiling where a block of threads computes a tile of output channels (e.g., 8 or 16) and a tile of spatial locations (e.g., 4x4 or 8x8). We load a slice of input into shared memory corresponding to the receptive fields of the tile, and load weights for the output channels into shared memory or registers. So threads in the same warp have different `oc` values. When they access `s_weight[oc_local][...]`, the index into shared memory is spaced by `IC*KH*KW = 363 floats`. The bank index is `(base + offset) % 32`. Since 363 % 32 = 11, consecutive threads (with oc_local differing by 1) access banks (base + 11*oc_local) % 32. Since 11 and 32 are coprime, threads in a warp access distinct banks. So no bank conflicts for weight reads. Great! When reading `s_input`, all threads in a warp access the same `ic` and `kh`, but different `kw`? Wait, `thread_local_ow` differs across threads. Since `thread_local_ow` varies, `tile_iw` varies. Because `tile_iw` is the last dimension and contiguous in memory, consecutive threads (varying `thread_local_ow`) access consecutive memory locations. This is a broadcast/sequential access pattern: threads with consecutive `thread_local_ow` read consecutive floats, which maps to consecutive banks (since bank = addr/4 % 32). For a warp of 32 threads, they read 32 consecutive words, which are in 32 different banks (if align). So no bank conflicts. Excellent. Thus the shared memory access is highly efficient. **Global memory access:** - Loading input: Each thread loads a few input elements. The access pattern: We linearize over `ic, ih, iw`. Thread i loads element i, i+512, etc. Since `iw` is the fastest varying dimension, consecutive threads load consecutive `iw`, which are contiguous in global memory. So loads are coalesced. - Loading weights: Linearize over `oc_local, ic, kh, kw`. Consecutive threads load consecutive `kw`, which are contiguous. Coalesced. **Output writes:** Each thread writes one output element. For a fixed `oc` and `oh`, varying `ow` writes consecutive memory (since W dimension is contiguous). Threads in a warp have varying `threadIdx.x` (ow) and same `threadIdx.y` (oh) and `threadIdx.z` (oc). Wait, if `blockDim.x = OW_TILE`, `blockDim.y = OH_TILE`, `blockDim.z = OC_TILE`. Then warps are formed by linearizing threadIdx in x, then y, then z. The exact mapping depends on the hardware, but typically threads are grouped by consecutive threadIdx values. If we linearize as `tid = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y*blockDim.x + threadIdx.z*blockDim.x*blockDim.y`, then a warp of 32 threads will first cover threadIdx.x (ow). So for a given oh and oc, 32 threads will have consecutive ow (if OW_TILE >= 32). But OW_TILE is only 4 or 8. So a warp will contain multiple oh and oc. Let's compute with BLOCK_OW=4, BLOCK_OH=4, BLOCK_OC=32. Then `blockDim.x=4`, `blockDim.y=4`, `blockDim.z=32`. `tid = x + y*4 + z*16`. A warp of 32 threads covers `tid` 0..31. That corresponds to z from 0 to 1 (since each z step is 16). For z=0: x=0..3, y=0..3 -> 16 threads. For z=1: x=0..3, y=0..3 -> next 16 threads. So a warp contains threads for two different oc values (0 and 1), all 16 spatial positions. When writing output, the output layout is NCHW: `y[n, oc, oh, ow]`. The stride for oc is `OH*OW`, for oh is `OW`, for ow is 1. So threads in the warp write to addresses separated by `OC_stride * 16` in terms of `tid`? Let's compute: - Thread (z=0, y=0, x=0): addr = base + 0*OH*OW + 0*OW + 0 - Thread (z=0, y=0, x=1): addr = base + 0 + 0 + 1 - ... - Thread (z=0, y=3, x=3): addr = base + 0 + 3*OW + 3 - Thread (z=1, y=0, x=0): addr = base + 1*OH*OW + 0 + 0 So within a warp, addresses are not fully contiguous. There are two groups of 16 addresses, each group contiguous, but the groups are separated by `OH*OW - 16` elements. For writing, this might cause some memory divergence but still coalesced within each half-warp? Actually, for Ampere/Hopper, memory transactions are warp-wide. If threads access two separated segments, the hardware may issue two transactions. But each segment is contiguous 16 floats (64 bytes). So it might issue two 64B transactions (or one 128B that covers both if close enough). `OH*OW = 55*55 = 3025`. The gap between the two segments is 3025 floats = 12100 bytes. That's far apart, so two separate transactions. This is okay but not perfect. To improve, we might want to choose block dimensions such that warps are aligned with contiguous output memory. Since output is NCHW, the most contiguous dimension is `ow`. We want threads in a warp to differ in `ow` as much as possible. But we also need to handle the case where `ow` tile is small (e.g., 4). So a warp will span multiple `oh` and `oc`. But the compute requires `oh` and `ow` to determine which input patch to read. However, we can compute `oh` and `ow` from `threadIdx.x` if we map them appropriately. The memory layout of output for a fixed `oc` is contiguous in `ow`, strided by `OW` for `oh`. With 32 spatial positions, they cover e.g., 8 ow across 4 oh (if BLOCK_OW=8, BLOCK_OH=4). The addresses are not strictly contiguous because moving from one row to the next jumps by `OW - BLOCK_OW`. But if `BLOCK_OW` aligns well, the warp covers a rectangular tile. Let's examine write patterns more carefully. For NCHW output, write address for thread (oc, oh, ow) is: `&y[n, oc, oh, ow] = y_base + n*OC*OH*OW + oc*OH*OW + oh*OW + ow`. If we have a warp of 32 threads, the addresses they access determine coalescing. Let's look at the case BLOCK_OH=2, BLOCK_OW=16, BLOCK_OC=32. `tid = x + y*16 + z*32`. Let's examine input load coalescing for BLOCK_OH=1, BLOCK_OW=32. Input indexing: global memory access for loading input tile: ``` global_h = oh_start*stride - pad + ih global_w = ow_start*stride - pad + iw ``` where `ih` ranges 0..10, `iw` ranges 0..134. For a fixed `ih` and `ic`, consecutive `iw` are contiguous in memory. Our loading loop linearizes over `ic, ih, iw`. The 1024 threads will load consecutive `iw` values. So loads are fully coalesced. Great. Weight load coalescing: Weight indexing: `w[global_oc, ic, kh, kw]`. Consecutive `kw` are contiguous. Our linearization over `oc_local, ic, kh, kw` means consecutive threads load consecutive `kw`. Fully coalesced. Compute pattern: Each thread computes: ``` float acc = bias[oc]; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { float a = s_input[ic * 11 * 135 + kh * 135 + (threadIdx.x * 4 + kw)]; float b = s_weight[oc_local * 363 + ic * 121 + kh * 11 + kw]; acc += a * b; } } } ``` Wait: `tile_iw = threadIdx.x * stride + kw`. stride=4. So `tile_iw = threadIdx.x * 4 + kw`. That means for threadIdx.x=0..31, we access `s_input` at positions 0..10, 4..14, 8..18, etc. Are these accesses coalesced in shared memory? Shared memory bank index = (addr/4) % 32. For fixed `ic` and `kh`, `base = ic*11*135 + kh*135`. For a given `kw`, threads access `base + threadIdx.x*4 + kw`. The addresses are spaced by 4 floats = 16 bytes. Bank index difference = (4) % 32 = 4. So consecutive threads access banks offset by 4. This is fine (no conflict) because 4 and 32 are not coprime? Actually, if 32 threads access addresses spaced by 4 words, the set of banks accessed will be 8 distinct banks (since gcd(4,32)=8), and 4 threads will map to the same bank. This causes 8-way bank conflicts! That's bad. Let's analyze carefully: For a warp of 32 threads, each thread accesses `s_input[base + threadIdx.x * 4 + kw]`. The shared memory word index is `base_word + threadIdx.x * 4 + kw`. Bank = (base_word + threadIdx.x*4 + kw) % 32. Since `threadIdx.x` increments by 1, bank increments by 4 mod 32. So the banks accessed are: bank0, bank4, bank8, bank12, bank16, bank20, bank24, bank28, bank0, bank4, ... So 32 threads map to only 8 banks, with 4 threads per bank. This is a 4-way bank conflict for shared memory. Because threads in a warp that access the same bank are serialized. This will hurt performance significantly. We need to avoid bank conflicts in shared memory reads of input and weights. **Fixing shared memory bank conflicts:** The conflict arises because stride=4, so the access pattern for `tile_iw` is not consecutive across threads within a warp. If we instead store the input tile in shared memory with the `w` dimension padded to avoid conflicts, we can fix this. Specifically, we want threadIdx.x to access consecutive words in shared memory for a given kw. But `tile_iw = threadIdx.x * stride + kw`. If we store `s_input` with a "swizzled" layout such that `s_input[ic][ih][tile_iw]` maps to banks based on `tile_iw`, then consecutive threads access `tile_iw` spaced by stride. To make them access consecutive banks, we could store the input tile with a transposition or padding. Alternatively, we can change the thread mapping. Instead of having `threadIdx.x` correspond to `ow`, we could have `threadIdx.x` correspond to `ow` but load input in a way that threads access consecutive `tile_iw`. But `tile_iw` depends on `ow*stride + kw`. If `ow` differs by 1, `tile_iw` differs by stride=4. So we can't change that. What if we assign each thread to compute all `ow` for a given `oc` and `oh`? Then threads in a warp would have same `ow` but different `oc`? No, that doesn't help. What if we restructure the loops? Since IC=3, KH=11, KW=11, we could unroll the loops and for each `kw`, have threads load `s_input[ic][ih][threadIdx.x*stride + kw]`. The bank conflict is inherent due to stride. We can pad the shared memory array: `s_input[IC][in_tile_h][in_tile_w + PAD]`. Then the address calculation includes padding. We want `bank = (base + threadIdx.x*stride + kw) % 32` to be distinct for each thread. Since `stride=4`, `gcd(stride,32)=4`, so we cannot get distinct banks by linear addressing. But if we store the data in a transposed layout: `s_input[ic][ih][kw][ow]`? Wait, we have a tile of ow that is 32 wide in output space, which corresponds to an input tile_w = 135. If we store the input tile with the `kw` dimension innermost and `ow` outer? Let's think. But the input data naturally comes as `[ic][ih][tile_iw]`. We could reorganize while loading: each thread loads a strip of 11 values (for kw=0..10) corresponding to its `ow`. Actually, for each `ic`, `ih`, and each `ow`, we need the 11 values that will be multiplied with the 11 weights for that `ow`. Specifically, for a given `ow`, the relevant input values for different `kw` are at `tile_iw = ow*stride + kw`. So we can store `s_patch[ic][ih][BLOCK_OW][KW]`. Then threadIdx.x indexes `ow` and we read `s_patch[ic][ih][threadIdx.x][kw]`. Shared memory addressing: `addr = ((ic*in_tile_h + ih)*BLOCK_OW + threadIdx.x)*KW + kw`. Bank = (addr/4) % 32. Since `KW=11`, consecutive threads (differing by `threadIdx.x`) have addresses spaced by `KW = 11` words. Bank difference = 11 % 32 = 11. Since gcd(11,32)=1, consecutive threads access distinct banks! So no bank conflict! Great. This is a key insight: we should restructure the shared memory for input to be `[IC][in_tile_h][BLOCK_OW][KW]`. But we need to populate it correctly from global memory. Alternatively, we can store as `[IC][in_tile_h][KW][BLOCK_OW]`. Then `addr` spacing per threadIdx.x is 1 word (contiguous), but we need to store by reading from global memory with stride. Let's think. If we store as `[IC][in_tile_h][KW][BLOCK_OW]`, then for a given `ic, ih, kw`, the `BLOCK_OW` values (one per `ow`) are contiguous. So loading from global memory is coalesced (since global memory `tile_iw` for consecutive `ow` are spaced by stride=4, not contiguous). No, for a fixed `ow`, the values `tile_iw = ow*stride + kw` for kw=0..10 are contiguous in global memory (since kw increments by 1). Let's define: ``` #define BLOCK_OW 32 #define BLOCK_OC 32 #define BLOCK_OH 1 // let's stick with 1 for perfect coalescing #define IC 3 #define KH 11 #define KW 11 #define STRIDE 4 // input tile: for each output ow, the input indices needed are ow*STRIDE + kw for kw in [0,KW) // So we can pre-fetch input as patch[IC][KH][BLOCK_OW][KW] // where patch[ic][kh][ow][kw] = input[n, ic, oh_start*STRIDE - PAD + kh, ow_start*STRIDE - PAD + ow*STRIDE + kw] // But ow_start*STRIDE - PAD + ow*STRIDE + kw = (ow_start + ow)*STRIDE - PAD + kw. ``` Wait, if we store patch in this layout, we no longer need to compute `ow*stride + kw` at compute time; we just read `patch[ic][kh][ow][kw]`. So in the compute loop, we just do: ``` for (int ic=0; ic<IC; ++ic) for (int kh=0; kh<KH; ++kh) for (int kw=0; kw<KW; ++kw) acc += patch[ic][kh][ow][kw] * weight[oc][ic][kh][kw]; ``` This is great because the access to `patch` for a given `kw` is `&patch[ic][kh][0][kw] + ow`, which is contiguous across `ow`. And in shared memory, since the inner dimension is `KW=11`, the stride between `ow` slices is `KW` words. Bank difference = 11. As noted, 11 is coprime to 32, so consecutive `ow` map to distinct banks. No conflicts! For weight access: `weight[oc][ic][kh][kw]`. For a fixed `ic,kh,kw`, threads with different `oc` (threadIdx.z) access `weight[oc0 + dz][ic][kh][kw]`. Address spacing is `IC*KH*KW = 363` words. Bank difference = 363 % 32 = 11. Again coprime to 32, so distinct banks. No conflicts! So with this layout, both input and weight shared memory accesses are conflict-free. Excellent. Now let's finalize the block shape. Option A: BLOCK_OH=1, BLOCK_OW=32, BLOCK_OC=32. Threads=1024. - Spatial tile: 1 row x 32 columns. - Output writes: contiguous 32 floats per warp, perfect coalescing. - Input patch shared mem: `float patch[3][11][32][11]`. Wait, but the weights are accessed sequentially by channel. If multiple threads in a warp access different output channels, they'll access adjacent weights. But each thread does 3 loads from adjacent memory. Since threads in a warp access consecutive addresses (if layout is OC-major), this would be perfectly coalesced! Weight layout in PyTorch: (out_channels, in_channels, kh, kw). So channel 0's weights are at [0, :, :, :], channel 1 at [1, :, :, :], etc. For thread cx computing channels [cx*3, cx*3+1, cx*3+2], in the inner loop over ic, kh, kw: - Thread cx loads weight[cx*3, ic, kh, kw], weight[cx*3+1, ic, kh, kw], weight[cx*3+2, ic, kh, kw]. Since these are separated by 3*11*11 = 363 floats, threads in a warp accessing their respective channels will access memory with a stride of 363 floats between consecutive threads. That's NOT coalesced! Oh wait. If thread 0 accesses [0, 0, 0, 0], thread 1 accesses [3, 0, 0, 0]. These are 3*11*11*4 = 5.8KB apart. For a warp reading 32 values, the addresses span 32*363*4 = 46KB. This is terrible for coalescing. We need to fix the thread assignment or the access pattern. Option A: Transpose the weights so that for a given (ic, kh, kw), all output channels are contiguous. But we can't change PyTorch's weight format easily. Option B: Have each thread compute channels that are adjacent. But 96/32 = 3, so each thread would compute 3 adjacent channels. Then in the access: Thread 0: [0, 0, 0, 0], [1, 0, 0, 0], [2, 0, 0, 0] (adjacent, coalesced within thread) Thread 1: [3, 0, 0, 0], [4, 0, 0, 0], [5, 0, 0, 0] ... Thread i: [i*3, 0, 0, 0], ... When 32 threads in a warp simultaneously execute the first load (channel i*3, ic=0, kh=0, kw=0), they access addresses: 0, 363, 726, ... apart. Stride of 363*4 = 1452 bytes. This is terrible for coalescing. To get coalesced access, all threads in a warp should access the same address (broadcast) or consecutive addresses. If all threads in a warp compute the same channel(s), they broadcast the weight loads. Good! But then we need many warps to cover all 96 channels. For 96 threads, that's a span of 139KB. This won't coalesce. However, on H100 with L2 cache, these will hit cache after the first block reads them. And since weights are only 139KB, the first block to read them will load into L2, and subsequent blocks will get L2 hits. L2 bandwidth is much higher than DRAM. Actually, the most straightforward approach: ```cuda __global__ void conv_kernel(float* out, float* in, float* w, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K, int OH, int OW) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * K * OH * OW; if (idx >= total) return; // Decompose idx into n, k, oh, ow int ow = idx % OW; int tmp = idx / OW; int oh = tmp % OH; tmp = tmp / OH; int k = tmp % K; int n = tmp / K; float sum = 0; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 11; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 11; kw++) { int h = oh * 4 + kh - 2; int w_in = ow * 4 + kw - 2; if (h >= 0 && h < H && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W) { float in_val = in[((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w_in]; float w_val = w[((k * C + c) * 11 + kh) * 11 + kw]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } out[((n * K + k) * OH + oh) * OW + ow] = sum; } ``` This is naive but maybe not terrible if weights stay in cache. The branch for bounds checking might hurt though, since only the edges need it. Looking at the problem again: - `nn.Conv2d(in_channels=3, out_channels=96, kernel_size=11, stride=4, padding=2)` - This is the first layer of AlexNet! AlexNet's first layer is notoriously difficult to optimize with standard GEMM because of the 3 input channels. In fact, custom direct convolutions for AlexNet's first layer are a well-known optimization target. For AlexNet layer 1, a common optimization is: 1. Process multiple output channels simultaneously 2. Exploit spatial parallelism 3. Since IC=3 is small, load the three input channels and do FMAs with multiple output channel weights I'll write a kernel where: - A warp (32 threads) handles 32 output channels for multiple spatial positions. - Each thread in the warp computes one output channel for some spatial positions. - Actually, to avoid the weight coalescing issue, let's have each thread compute one output channel for multiple spatial positions, and have the warp cover 32 output channels. Within a warp: - Thread 0 computes output channel 0 for spatial positions p0, p1, ... - Thread 1 computes output channel 1 for spatial positions p0, p1, ... - All threads in warp process the same set of spatial positions simultaneously. - For a given (kh, kw, ic), all threads load the same input value (since they process the same spatial position) - BROADCAST, perfect! - Then each thread loads its own weight for its output channel. - Weight loads: thread i loads weight[i, ic, kh, kw]. Since i varies across warp, this is strided access with stride 363. Bad coalescing. Alternative: All threads in warp compute the SAME channel. - Thread i computes channel k for spatial position i. - For a given (kh, kw, ic), all threads load different inputs (coalesced, since spatial positions are adjacent in memory if we arrange them right). - Then all threads load the SAME weight (broadcast). Good! - But we only cover 1 channel with 32 threads. To cover 96 channels, we need 96 warps, or run this 96 times. With 96 warps = 3072 threads. Maybe organize into blocks of 3072 threads? NVIDIA shared memory has 32 banks. A 4-way conflict means 4 serial accesses, each reading from 8 banks simultaneously. This is a performance hit, but maybe not catastrophic. For kw=0: thread t accesses w_t = ow_t*4 + 0. These are strided. For kw=1: thread t accesses w_t+1. These are also strided by 4, but offset by 1. So for each kw, the warp does 4 memory transactions. With 11 kw values, that's 44 transactions per (ic, kh). For the whole kernel: 3 * 11 * 44 = 1,452 input transactions per warp. Plus 363 weight transactions. At L2 bandwidth, this might be fine. But at L1, if the region is cached, each transaction is an L1 hit. I just realized I should verify if L1 cache can actually help. The first access for a given (ic, h, w) will be an L1 miss (if not already cached). Subsequent accesses to the same location by the same warp will be hits. But in our kernel, does a warp access the same input location multiple times? For a given thread, it accesses a unique (h, w) for each (oh, ow, kh, kw). So no reuse within a single thread. But between threads in a warp: thread t1 with (oh1, ow1) and thread t2 with (oh2, ow2) might access the same input pixel for some (kh1, kw1) and (kh2, kw2). Example: thread at (oh, ow) with kh=1, kw=1 accesses h=oh*4+1, w=ow*4+1. Thread at (oh, ow+1) with kh=1, kw=-3? No, kw ranges 0..10. Thread at (oh, ow) with kh=5, kw=5 accesses h+5, w+5. Thread at (oh, ow+1) with kh=5, kw=1 accesses same h+5, w+5 (since (ow+1)*4+1 = ow*4+5). Yes! There is reuse between threads! Actually, the receptive fields overlap by 7 pixels (kernel=11, stride=4). So adjacent output pixels (offset by 1 in ow) share 7 input columns. Over the 32 positions in the warp, there's substantial overlap. But threads access different (h, w) at different times because (kh, kw) loop iterates sequentially. Thread t1 accesses (h_t1, w_t1) at iteration (kh, kw). Thread t2 accesses (h_t2, w_t2) at the same iteration. These might or might not be the same memory address. When are they the same? When oh1*4 + kh1 = oh2*4 + kh2 and ow1*4 + kw1 = ow2*4 + kw2. If ow2 = ow1 + 1, then we need ow1*4 + kw1 = (ow1+1)*4 + kw2, so kw1 = 4 + kw2. Since kw1, kw2 in [0, 10], this means kw2 = kw1 - 4. So for kw1 in [4, 10], there's overlap with the neighbor at kw2 = kw1 - 4. This is small-scale accumulation in time and space. Actually, for a whole warp covering 32 output positions (4x8 tile), the total input region is about 22x38. The 32 threads iterate over the 11x11 kernel. Over the 121 iterations of (kh, kw), the 32 threads access 32 different points in each iteration. They sweep through the 22x38 region. Since the region is only 2,508 floats, and a warp accesses it over 363*32 = 11,616 accesses, there's clearly reuse. But the accesses are scattered in time. The L1 cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. When a thread accesses a float, the entire cache line is loaded. A cache line covers 32 contiguous w values for a fixed h, ic. The warp accesses 8 different w values (separated by 4) in each of 4 rows. So a single warp transaction only uses 8 out of 32 floats in the cache line. But the other 24 floats are loaded into L1. These 24 floats might be used by other threads in subsequent iterations. This means L1 hit rate could actually be quite good! OK, let me just write the kernel and test it. Before writing the final code, let me draft the CUDA kernel. I'll also check if using float4 or other vector types helps. For weights, since we have broadcast, vector types don't help. For input, each thread loads one float at a time. With 32 threads, they do 32 simultaneous loads. Could we use float4 to load 4 contiguous floats per thread? But each thread only needs 1 float per (kh, kw) iteration. It would need 4 floats for 4 consecutive kw iterations. If we load float4, we can reuse it for kw, kw+1, kw+2, kw+3. But then we'd need to handle the loop differently. And the alignment: ow*4+kw needs to be aligned to 4 for float4 load. ow*4 is always a multiple of 4, and kw varies. For kh=0, kw=0: w = ow*4. This is aligned to 4! For kh=0, kw=1: w = ow*4+1. Not aligned. So only kw=0 is aligned for float4. Hmm. Actually, `((n * 3 + ic) * 228 + h) * 228 + w`. w = ow*4 + kw. If kw=0, w is a multiple of 4. So we can use float4 loads when kw starts at 0 and increments by 4. But the inner loop would need to change. We could restructure: For each (ic, kh), iterate kw in steps of 4: load float4 at kw, kw+1, kw+2, kw+3 compute 4 FMAs This would reduce load instructions by 4x for input. But the float4 isn't perfectly used if we just need 4 separate floats. The compiler might already vectorize this. Actually, for global memory loads with strided access, float4 doesn't help because the loads are not contiguous across threads. Let me just write a clean kernel without premature optimization. We can tune based on profiling. Here's my plan: 1. Write the kernel with pre-padding and the block layout described. 2. Compile and check correctness. 3. Profile to see bottlenecks. 4. Optimize if needed. Let me code it up now. Actually, one more idea. What if we unroll the ic loop (3 iterations) and the kh loop (11 iterations) partially? We can use `#pragma unroll` for small loops. ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < 3; ++ic) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 11; ++kh) { int h = base_h + kh; float in_row_base = in_ptr[ic * 228 * 228 + h * 228 + base_w]; // But base_w varies per thread... } } ``` Each thread has its own base_w. Each output position requires reading 3 * 11 * 11 = 363 input values, but these come from a 11x11 window in the input image, strided by 4. For memory coalescing, if adjacent threads handle adjacent output channels (N dimension), they all read the SAME input window but different weights. This is great for weight coalescing (weights for adjacent channels are adjacent in memory), and they all read the same input values which can be broadcast. Wait, reading the same input: - If threads in a warp are computing the same spatial position but different output channels: - They all load the same input patch (can't coalesce perfectly but can be cached/L1 broadcast) - They load different weights (adjacent channels are adjacent in weight memory, so coalesced!) This seems like a great pattern! Let me create a kernel where: - Each warp computes one spatial position and 32 output channels - But N=96, so 3 warps per spatial position cover all 96 channels Grid: - Block = 32 threads (1 warp) or 96 threads (3 warps) - If 96 threads: each block computes 1 spatial position x 96 channels - Grid size = N * out_h * out_w = 256 * 55 * 55 = 774,400 blocks That's too many blocks for good occupancy. Actually 774k blocks is fine for latency hiding, but we'd like more work per thread to amortize block launch overhead. Let's increase to 8 spatial positions per block: - Block = 96 threads x 8 warps? No, let's do 96 threads (computing all channels for 1 spatial position), and each block computes 8 spatial positions sequentially or with 8 blocks of 96. Actually, let's use a 2D grid where: - BlockDim.x = 32 (channels in groups of 32) - BlockDim.y = 8 (multiple spatial positions) Total = 256 threads per block Each block computes 8 spatial positions x 32 channels. Grid: - x = (96/32) = 3 channel groups - y = out_h * out_w / 8 spatial groups (rounded up) - z = N = 256 batches Total blocks = 3 * (55*55/8≈379) * 256 = 291,072 blocks Each thread computes 1 output value. Kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_kernel(...) { int n = blockIdx.z; int spatial_idx = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int c = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int oh = spatial_idx / out_w; int ow = spatial_idx % out_w; if (oh >= out_h || ow >= out_w || c >= K) return; float acc = bias[c]; int base_h = oh * stride; int base_w = ow * stride; for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 11; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 11; ++kw) { float in = input[...]; // same for all threads in a warp that have same threadIdx.y but different threadIdx.x float w = weight[...]; // coalesced across threadIdx.x acc += in * w; } } } output[...] = acc; } ``` Wait, the input load is the same for all threads with the same `threadIdx.y` but different `threadIdx.x`. In a warp, there are 32 threads with different `threadIdx.x` but same `threadIdx.y`... actually in my layout, a warp would have 32 threads in x direction (threadIdx.x varying, threadIdx.y constant). Yes! So all 32 threads in a warp load the same input element simultaneously. This causes broadcast from L1 cache, which is excellent! And weight loads are coalesced across the 32 threads (adjacent channels). This seems like a good design. Let me write it up. But wait, threadIdx.x = 32 and threadIdx.y = 8 means a warp has 32 threads in x and 1 in y. So 1 warp handles 1 spatial position and 32 channels. Perfect. Let me write the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_kernel(const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int N, int in_h, int in_w, int out_h, int out_w) { const int n = blockIdx.z; const int c = blockIdx.x * 32 + threadIdx.x; const int spatial = blockIdx.y * 8 + threadIdx.y; const int oh = spatial / out_w; const int ow = spatial % out_w; if (n >= N || c >= 96 || oh >= out_h || ow >= out_w) return; const int base_h = oh * 4; const int base_w = ow * 4; float acc = bias[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < 3; ++ic) { const float* in_base = input + ((n * 3 + ic) * in_h + base_h) * in_w + base_w; const float* w_base = weight + ((c * 3 + ic) * 11 + 0) * 11 + 0; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 11; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 11; ++kw) { float in_val = in_base[kh * in_w + kw]; float w_val = w_base[kh * 11 + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } output[(((n * 96) + c) * out_h + oh) * out_w + ow] = acc; } ``` Wait, weight access: `weight[((c * 3 + ic) * 11 + kh) * 11 + kw]` For c varying across threads in a warp, adjacent threads access adjacent memory: coalesced. Good. Input access: `input[((n * 3 + ic) * in_h + base_h + kh) * in_w + base_w + kw]` For threads with different c but same spatial position, they all access the SAME input element. This is a broadcast, which is fast if in L1. Good. But this kernel has a huge issue: each thread does 363 multiply-adds sequentially. The loop isn't parallelized within the thread. For the 11x11 loops, we could unroll them completely. 121 iterations of the innermost, times 3 = 363. Unrolling 363 iterations won't help much. Let me try a different approach: interleave multiple output computations so the compiler can pipeline better. Or better yet, process multiple spatial positions per thread. Instead of 8 positions per block with 8 threads in y, what if each thread handles 4 spatial positions and we have fewer threads? Actually, let me think about register-level parallelism. Each thread computes one output element. To improve throughput, we can have each thread compute multiple output elements to allow instruction-level parallelism. Option: each thread computes T outputs (different spatial positions, same channels). This way, we can interleave loads and FMAs. Let's say each thread computes 4 outputs at different spatial positions. Then: - BlockDim.x = 32 (channels), BlockDim.y = 2 - Each block: 32 * 2 = 64 threads, computes 32 channels x 8 spatial positions - But each thread does 4 * 363 = 1452 FMAs Actually, a better approach: have each thread compute a tile of outputs, e.g., 2x2 spatial positions x some channels. Let me reconsider. On H100, a thread can do ~1 FMA per clock. With 1452 FMAs, at 2GHz that's 0.7 microseconds per thread. With 256 threads per SM and 132 SMs, full utilization gives ~34k threads * 0.7μs = ~24 microseconds total. That's not too bad. But we also need to account for memory bandwidth. Each thread reads 3*11*11 input values and 3*11*11 weight values. Since weights are different per channel, total memory: - Inputs: ~151 MB (but heavily reused across channels) - Weights: 96 * 3 * 11 * 11 * 4 bytes = ~139 KB - Outputs: ~283 MB With the broadcast pattern, 32 threads in a warp share the same input. So input bandwidth is reduced by 32x. Weight bandwidth is the main concern, which is 139 KB - negligible! So this kernel is actually very good for memory bandwidth. The main concern is arithmetic throughput and instruction latency. Let me try to pipeline better. Each thread computes 1 output but with loop unrolling and good compiler optimization, it should be decent. Actually, I know a better approach for Hopper: use warp-group matrix multiply (wgmma) through PTX. But that's very complex and might not work well for small K=363. Let me also consider using float4/vector loads. For weights, adjacent channels are accessed, so float4 would load 4 adjacent channels' weights. Let me write a more optimized version: ```cuda __global__ void conv_kernel(...) { // Each warp handles 32 channels and 1 spatial position // Block: 4 warps x 32 threads = 128 threads // Block handles 4 spatial positions, 32 channels each // But we can cover 96 channels with 3 blocks in x dimension int n = blockIdx.z; int sp = blockIdx.y * 4 + (threadIdx.x / 32); // which spatial position within block int c = blockIdx.x * 32 + (threadIdx.x % 32); // ... compute ... } ``` Wait this is getting messy. Let me try another layout: Grid: z=N, y=out_h*out_w, x=96/32=3 Block: 32 threads Each block handles: 1 spatial position, 32 channels, 1 batch. Total blocks: 256 * 3025 * 3 = 2,323,200 blocks That's a LOT of blocks. Launch overhead might be significant. At ~1-2 microseconds per kernel launch overhead, and 2.3M blocks... Wait no, on H100 with SMs processing blocks concurrently, it's more about occupancy. With 128 threads per block and 132 SMs, max concurrent blocks is 132 * (max blocks per SM, say 16 if 128 threads) = 2112 concurrently. But there are 2.3M total blocks, so the queue is very deep, allowing latency hiding. But each block only does 363 FMAs * 32 threads = 11,616 FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS, 11616 FMAs is ~174 FLOPs per block, which is tiny. The arithmetic density is very low per block. To amortize better, I should have larger blocks doing more work. Let me try a different approach: process multiple spatial positions per thread. Grid: z=N, y=(out_h*out_w)/4, x=96/32=3 Block: 128 threads Each block handles 4 spatial positions x 32 channels. Threads: 128 = 32 * 4, where 4 warps each handle 1 spatial position. Within each warp, 32 threads handle 32 channels. Total blocks: 256 * 756 * 3 = 580,608 blocks. Still a lot but better. Actually let me think about this more carefully. On modern GPUs, block overhead is very small. The main issue is whether we can keep the SMs busy. With 3025 spatial positions per image and 256 batches, there are 774,400 independent "spatial tasks." Each task needs to compute 96 channels. If one block computes 32 channels for 1 spatial position, that's 32 threads doing useful work. If a block computes 32*8 = 256 channels for 1 spatial position... but N=96. What if a block computes 96 channels for 8 spatial positions? - 96 threads, each handling 1 channel and 8 spatial positions sequentially - Or 96*8 threads handling all combinations Let me try: 96 threads per block. - Each thread handles 1 channel and some number of spatial positions. - For 8 spatial positions per block and 96 channels: 96 threads, each doing 8 * 363 = 2904 FMAs. ```cuda __global__ void conv_kernel(...) { int n = blockIdx.z; int c = threadIdx.x; // 0..95 int base_spatial = blockIdx.y * 8; float acc[8]; #pragma unroll for (int s = 0; s < 8; ++s) { int spatial = base_spatial + s; int oh = spatial / out_w; int ow = spatial % out_w; if (oh < out_h && ow < out_w) { acc[s] = bias[c]; } } // ... } ``` Hmm, with 96 threads, we get 96-way parallel on channels but sequential on spatial positions. Actually, the best might be to treat this as a GEMM problem and use an existing library. But we need custom CUDA. Let me try writing a kernel where each thread computes a small tile of outputs, doing implicit GEMM with some shared memory staging for the reduction dimension. Consider: C (output) = A (M_row x K) @ B (N_col x K)^T For each output tile, we need to sum over K=363. Strategy: - Load a chunk of K (say 64) into shared memory for both A and B tiles - Compute partial sums for the tile of outputs - Repeat for all K chunks Tile shapes: - M_tile = 64 (output spatial positions) - N_tile = 8 (output channels) - K_tile = 32 (input features) Threads = 256. This is a standard GEMM approach. Let me implement it. But wait, A is the "input patches" which aren't stored explicitly. We'd need to compute the patch indices on the fly or precompute them. For implicit GEMM, the patch indices depend on the spatial position, and the input values are scattered in memory (from the input image). The layout isn't contiguous, so loading A into shared memory is complex. Alternative: direct convolution with shared memory for inputs. What if we tile the input spatially? - For a tile of output positions (e.g., 4x4 = 16 positions), the corresponding input region is roughly 4*stride x 4*stride = up to 47x47 (plus kernel overlap). - With C=3, that's 3 * 47 * 47 = 6627 floats = 26 KB. Fits in shared memory. - We can load this input tile once and reuse it for all channels. Actually, let me compute more carefully. For output tile (4x4) with stride 4 and kernel 11: - Output rows: 0,1,2,3 -> input rows: 0..14, 4..18, 8..22, 12..26 = overall 0..26 - Output cols: 0,1,2,3 -> input cols: 0..14, 4..18, 8..22, 12..26 = overall 0..26 - So we need input region 27x27 = 729 elements per channel, with C=3 that's 2187 = 8.5 KB If the tile is 8x8 outputs: - Input region: 0..50 in h and w = 51x51 = 2601 per channel, with C=3 = 7803 = 30.5 KB Both fit in shared memory. But threads accessing shared memory might have bank conflicts. A 4x4 output tile (16 outputs) with 96 channels: 1536 outputs per tile. With 256 threads: each thread computes 6 outputs. That's decent. Let me design the kernel: - Block handles 1 output tile (tile_oh x tile_ow) and all 96 channels - Load input region for the tile into shared memory - Each thread computes some output elements Actually, for the simplest possible fast kernel, let me try the "broadcast input / coalesced weight" approach first and see how it performs. Then I can iterate. Let me implement this cleanly: ```python cuda_code = """ __global__ void conv_forward_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int in_h, int in_w, int out_h, int out_w) { // Grid: (ch_block, sp_block, batch) // blockDim.x = 32 threads per warp // Each warp handles 1 spatial position and 32 channels const int n = blockIdx.z; const int c = blockIdx.x * 32 + threadIdx.x; const int spatial_idx = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; const int oh = spatial_idx / out_w; const int ow = spatial_idx % out_w; if (n >= N || c >= 96 || oh >= out_h || ow >= out_w) return; const int base_h = oh * 4; // stride = 4 const int base_w = ow * 4; float acc = bias[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < 3; ++ic) { const float* in_ptr = input + ((n * 3 + ic) * in_h + base_h) * in_w + base_w; const float* w_ptr = weight + ((c * 3 + ic) * 11) * 11; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 11; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 11; ++kw) { acc += in_ptr[kh * in_w + kw] * w_ptr[kh * 11 + kw]; } } } output[(((n * 96) + c) * out_h + oh) * out_w + ow] = acc; } """ ``` Block config: - blockDim = (32, 8, 1) -> 256 threads (8 warps) - Each block handles 8 spatial positions x 32 channels - Grid: (3, (55*55+7)/8=379, 256) Wait, 55*55 = 3025, /8 = 379 (rounded up). 3 * 379 * 256 = 291,072 blocks. Each warp handles 1 spatial position. 8 warps in a block handle 8 spatial positions. Actually with blockDim.y = 8, threads in a warp have: - threadIdx.x = 0..31, threadIdx.y = constant within a warp - Wait, in CUDA, threadIdx.x is the fastest varying dimension. A warp contains 32 consecutive threadIdx.x values with the same threadIdx.y and threadIdx.z. So if blockDim.x = 32 and blockDim.y = 8: - Warp 0: threadIdx.x = 0..31, threadIdx.y = 0 - Warp 1: threadIdx.x = 0..31, threadIdx.y = 1 - etc. Each warp has threads with the SAME threadIdx.y but DIFFERENT threadIdx.x. - Same spatial position (since spatial_idx depends on blockIdx.y and threadIdx.y) - Different channels (c depends on threadIdx.x) This means all 32 threads in the warp read the SAME input values but DIFFERENT weight values. Input is broadcast, weights are coalesced. Perfect! But there's a catch: the compiler might not understand that all threads in the warp read the same input, so it might generate separate load instructions instead of using L1 broadcast. But since they're exactly the same address, the hardware should handle it. For this specific 11x11 case, we can further optimize by preloading input into registers or shared memory to fully eliminate redundant loads. But with L1 broadcast, it might already be fast enough. Let me write this kernel and compile it. Actually, for even better performance, I could have each thread compute 2 or more output channels. But then adjacent threads wouldn't load adjacent weights, hurting coalescing. Hmm, let me think about the channel dimension. CuDNN typically stores weights as K x C x H x W. So weight[c1] and weight[c1+1] are separated by C*H*W = 363 elements. Adjacent channels are NOT adjacent in memory if we index as weight[c][ic][kh][kw]! Wait, PyTorch's Conv2d weight format is [out_channels, in_channels, kernel_h, kernel_w] = [96, 3, 11, 11]. So weight[c][ic][kh][kw] has stride: - stride between c and c+1: 3 * 11 * 11 = 363 So `weight + (c * 3 + ic) * 121` gives the base for channel c, input channel ic. For thread c and c+1 in the same warp, their weight bases differ by 363 floats = 363 * 4 = 1452 bytes. Since a warp loads 128 bytes at a time (32 threads * 4 bytes), these are far apart. NOT coalesced! Oh no! My assumption about weight coalescing was wrong because I forgot the 3 * 11 * 11 multiplier. But wait - 363 floats = 363 * 4 = 1452 bytes. The L1 cache line is 128 bytes. So each thread's weight access touches a different cache line. That's bad. However, for the broadcast input access, all threads hit the same cache line, so that's good. To coalesce weight loads, we should have threads within a warp access the SAME weight element at the same time (or adjacent elements within a cache line). This means threads should iterate over different spatial positions but the SAME channel, not different channels. So the better layout is: - Threads in a warp handle the SAME channel but DIFFERENT spatial positions - Weight is broadcast (loaded once per warp) - Input can be coalesced if spatial positions are adjacent Let me redesign: Grid: (c_block, sp_block, batch) - blockDim.x = 32 threads in a warp, all handling the same channel but different spatial positions - Each thread handles 1 spatial position - If we need 8 spatial positions per block, blockDim.y = 1, but we just have 32 spatial positions in x dimension. Actually, let's say: - blockDim = (32, 1, 1) - Grid: ((96+31)/32=3, (3025+31)/32=95, 256) Each block: 32 threads, each computes 1 spatial position x ~32 channels? No, let's have each block compute all channels for 32 spatial positions. Wait, let me be clearer. If threads in a warp all handle the same channel: - c = some channel - spatial_idx varies across threads = blockIdx.y * 32 + threadIdx.x For adjacent threadIdx.x values, spatial_idx values differ by 1. Adjacent spatial positions in the same row are adjacent in memory for the same channel. So input loads for adjacent spatial positions might be coalesced if they fall in the same row (which they do for most of the 32 positions if ow wraps within a row). Actually for a 1D spatial index, adjacent values might cross row boundaries at ow=55, causing some non-coalescing. But mostly coalesced. So layout: ```cuda const int n = blockIdx.z; const int c = blockIdx.x; const int spatial_idx = blockIdx.y * 32 + threadIdx.x; ``` But now each block only handles 1 channel and 32 spatial positions. Let me write this: ```cuda __global__ void conv_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int in_h, int in_w, int out_h, int out_w) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int n = blockIdx.z; const int c_off = tid >> 5; // 0..7 const int s_off = tid & 31; // 0..31 const int c = blockIdx.x * 8 + c_off; const int spatial_idx = blockIdx.y * 32 + s_off; if (c >= 96 || spatial_idx >= out_h * out_w) return; const int oh = spatial_idx / out_w; const int ow = spatial_idx % out_w; const int base_h = oh * 4; const int base_w = ow * 4; float acc = bias[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < 3; ++ic) { const float* w_ptr = weight + ((c * 3 + ic) * 11) * 11; const float* in_ptr = input + ((n * 3 + ic) * in_h + base_h) * in_w + base_w; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 11; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 11; ++kw) { acc += in_ptr[kh * in_w + kw] * w_ptr[kh * 11 + kw]; } } } output[(((n * 96 + c) * out_h) + oh) * out_w + ow] = acc; } ``` Wait, `in_ptr[kh * in_w + kw]` - adjacent threads (same warp, different s_off) have different `base_w`, so they access `in_ptr[0]`, `in_ptr[1]`, `in_ptr[2]`, etc (for different spatial positions). For adjacent ow values, base_w differs by 4, so the input addresses differ by 4 floats = 16 bytes. But since spatial_idx values are 0..31 for a warp, and ow varies from 0 to 31, after oh wraps the addresses get messy. Hmm wait, in my layout, `spatial_idx = blockIdx.y * 32 + s_off`. For blockIdx.y = 0, spatial_idx = 0..31, which means oh = 0, ow = 0..31. base_w = 0, 4, 8, ..., 124. So the input addresses differ by 16 bytes per thread. This is perfectly coalesced! For blockIdx.y = 1, spatial_idx = 32..63, so oh could be 0 or 1: - spatial_idx = 32 -> oh = 0, ow = 32 - spatial_idx = 55 -> oh = 0, ow = 55 - spatial_idx = 56 -> oh = 1, ow = 0 Oh no! When spatial_idx crosses the boundary of ow=out_w=55, the addresses jump from base_w=55*4=220 to base_h=56 (next row, base_w=0). The addresses are no longer contiguous. For spatial_idx = 55 (oh=0, ow=55), input address is at (base_h=0, base_w=220) For spatial_idx = 56 (oh=1, ow=0), input address is at (base_h=4, base_w=0) These are NOT contiguous in memory! Actually, for blockIdx.y, each block handles 32 consecutive spatial_idx values. Each boundary affects at most one group. But within an affected group, only some threads have non-contiguous addresses. Is there a better spatial grouping? What if I tile in 2D instead of 1D? Grid over (tile_oh, tile_ow) where: - tile_oh = some height - tile_ow = 32 (or a warp size) Then within a tile, all positions are in the same row or adjacent rows in a controlled way. ```cuda const int tile_ow = 32; const int tile_oh = 4; const int num_tiles_ow = (out_w + tile_ow - 1) / tile_ow; // 2 const int num_tiles_oh = (out_h + tile_oh - 1) / tile_oh; // 14 // Grid: (c_group, tile_oh * tile_ow_group, batch) // Wait, this gets complicated. // Alternative: const int tile_spatial = tile_oh * tile_ow; // 4 * 32 = 128 // Block handles 128 spatial positions, arranged as 4 rows x 32 columns. // But out_w = 55, so each "row" of 32 covers part of the output row. // Hmm, still awkward. ``` Actually, let me just proceed with the 1D spatial grouping and live with the occasional non-coalescing at row boundaries. It's likely still much better than any naive approach. For even better performance, what if I rearrange to process 2D tiles where each warp handles 32 positions in the spatial dimension, but I process the output width in chunks of 32? out_w = 55, so chunks of 32 gives: first chunk ow=0..31, second chunk ow=32..54 (plus padding). For the first chunk, all 32 positions are in the same row. For the second chunk (23 positions), still in the same row. That's perfect! I can structure the grid as: - For each output row (oh = 0..54), process ow in chunks of 32. - 2 chunks per row. - Total chunks = 55 * 2 = 110. - Grid y = 110 (spatial chunks). Then within each chunk, a warp handles all valid positions in that chunk. For chunk 0 (ow=0..31), all 32 threads are active. For chunk 1 (ow=32..54), only threads 0..22 are active (23 valid positions), thread 23..31 are idle. Grid: (12 channel groups, 110 spatial chunks, 256 batches) Block: 256 threads = 8 warps per block, each warp handles 8 channels x 1 spatial chunk? No. If block has 8 warps and handles 8 channels: - Each warp handles 1 channel and 1 spatial chunk. - Warp 0: channel base + 0, spatial chunk - Warp 1: channel base + 1, spatial chunk - etc. So block handles 8 channels and 1 spatial chunk. Total blocks = 12 * 110 * 256 = 337,920. Hmm, each block is quite small (only 1 spatial chunk = up to 32 positions x 8 channels = 256 outputs, handled by 256 threads = 1 output per thread). Wait, that's 1 output per thread, which means 363 FMAs per thread. Actually, let me calculate: 32 spatial positions in a chunk x 8 channels = 256 outputs. With 256 threads, each thread computes exactly 1 output. Each output requires 363 FMAs. So each thread does 363 FMAs. But for chunks with only 23 valid positions, 23*8=184 threads are active and 72 are idle. That's some wasted work but not terrible. For channel c=0, spatial chunk=0 (ow=0..31): - All 32 threads in warp 0 are active. - Each thread computes spatial position (oh, ow) for its threadIdx.x. - Input loads are contiguous (ow varies by 1, base_w varies by 4, addresses differ by 16 bytes = coalesced). - Weight loads are broadcast (all threads load weights for c=0). This is very good! Let me write this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int in_h, int in_w, int out_h, int out_w) { const int n = blockIdx.z; const int c = blockIdx.x * 8 + (threadIdx.x >> 5); // 8 warps, 8 channels const int chunk_idx = blockIdx.y; const int oh = chunk_idx / 2; // each row has 2 chunks const int chunk_in_row = chunk_idx % 2; const int ow = chunk_in_row * 32 + (threadIdx.x & 31); if (c >= 96 || oh >= out_h || ow >= out_w) return; const int base_h = oh * 4; const int base_w = ow * 4; float acc = bias[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < 3; ++ic) { const float* w_ptr = weight + ((c * 3 + ic) * 11) * 11; const float* in_ptr = input + ((n * 3 + ic) * in_h + base_h) * in_w + base_w; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 11; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 11; ++kw) { acc += in_ptr[kh * in_w + kw] * w_ptr[kh * 11 + kw]; } } } output[(((n * 96 + c) * out_h) + oh) * out_w + ow] = acc; } ``` Wait, but with blockDim.x = 256, warps are threadIdx.x = 0..31, 32..63, etc. Thread 0..31 have c = blockIdx.x * 8 + 0 (all same channel). Thread 32..63 have c = blockIdx.x * 8 + 1 (all same channel). ... Thread 224..255 have c = blockIdx.x * 8 + 7. For warp 0 (threads 0..31): - c = blockIdx.x * 8 + 0 (same for all) - ow = chunk_in_row * 32 + (0..31) = contiguous within chunk Yes! Weight broadcast, input coalesced. Perfect. Let me verify the output indexing: output[n][c][oh][ow] = output[((n * 96 + c) * out_h + oh) * out_w + ow] For adjacent threads in warp 0 (same c, different ow): - Addresses differ by 1 output element = 4 bytes. Coalesced write! ✓ For adjacent warps (same spatial, different c): - Addresses differ by out_h * out_w = 3025 elements = ~12KB. Not coalesced across warps, but that's fine. Wait, actually in the thread block, threads are grouped by c within each warp. So warp 0 writes channels [base_c] for spatial positions [ow=0..31], warp 1 writes channels [base_c+1] for spatial positions [ow=0..31], etc. The writes for warp 0 are contiguous (4 bytes apart). The writes for warp 1 are 3025*4 = 12100 bytes after warp 0's first write. Not contiguous with warp 0, but that's fine - each warp's writes are coalesced within itself. This looks good. Let me code it up and test. Actually, wait. I realize that `chunk_idx / 2` and `chunk_idx % 2` are integer operations. With 110 chunks, oh = 0..54, chunk_in_row = 0..1. But what about when chunk_idx is odd vs even? Let me verify: - chunk_idx 0: oh=0, chunk=0 -> ow=0..31 - chunk_idx 1: oh=0, chunk=1 -> ow=32..63 (but capped at 55) - chunk_idx 2: oh=1, chunk=0 -> ow=0..31 Wait, I made a mistake. 55 rows with 2 chunks each = 110 chunks. The formula is: oh = chunk_idx / 2 chunk_in_row = chunk_idx % 2 Yes, this works for 0..109. But the issue is that `oh` increments every 2 chunks. So the first two chunks correspond to row 0, next two to row 1, etc. With this layout, all threads in a block are computing positions in the SAME output row. Good for locality. Let me now think about whether there are any bank conflicts or other issues. Actually, one more optimization: loop unrolling. The compiler might not fully unroll 11x11=121 iterations by default. I'll add `#pragma unroll` to help. Also, I should consider that loading from `in_ptr[kh * in_w + kw]` for each kh, kw involves pointer arithmetic that the compiler can optimize. Let me also think about register usage. Each thread needs: - A few integers for indexing - `acc` (1 float) - Loop counters (optimized away with unrolling) - Some pointer values This should use very few registers, allowing high occupancy. One concern: the input values for different kh are loaded for each kw loop. With 11x11=121 loads per output, and 32 threads in a warp doing mostly the same loads except for the base_w offset, L1 cache should help. But 121 loads x 32 threads x 4 bytes = 15.5KB of memory traffic per warp per ic. Hmm, that's a lot. Wait, actually 121 loads x 4 bytes x 32 threads = 15.5KB, but many of these are from the same cache lines! Adjacent threads (different ow) load inputs that are offset by 4 floats. For kw=0, thread 0 loads in_ptr[0], thread 1 loads in_ptr[4], ..., thread 31 loads in_ptr[124]. These span 128 floats = 512 bytes = 4 cache lines (128 bytes each). Still some coalescing but threads load from different cache lines. Actually each load from a warp accesses one cache line (128 bytes). With 32 threads requesting 512 bytes contiguously, it takes 4 cache line loads (4 x 128 bytes). But wait,相邻线程访问相距 16 bytes, so 32个线程 -> 512 bytes -> 4 cache lines. GPU can load up to 32 bytes per thread per instruction? No, typically a warp can load from 1 to 4 cache lines depending on the access pattern. For now, let me just implement this and see how it performs. I can iterate. Also, I should note that PyTorch's own conv2d for this case is likely using cuDNN, which is extremely well-optimized. Achieving a speedup might require exceptional kernel design. But let's see. Let me try a different approach: using CUTLASS or implicit GEMM. For this specific problem size, an implicit GEMM kernel might be more competitive. Actually, let me look at whether cuDNN is already doing implicit GEMM. For small kernels and small batch sizes, direct conv can be faster. For large batch sizes (256 here), implicit GEMM usually wins. Wait, another thought: what if I use torch's matmul instead of a custom kernel? I can implement implicit GEMM in PyTorch: ```python # Unfold input to patches patches = F.unfold(x, kernel_size=11, stride=4, padding=2) # shape: [N, C*K*K, out_h*out_w] # Reshape weight: [96, 3*11*11] weight_mat = self.conv1.weight.view(96, -1) # Matmul out = weight_mat @ patches # [96, N*out_h*out_w] out = out.view(N, 96, out_h, out_w) ``` But F.unfold + matmul might not be faster than cuDNN conv2d because unfold itself is expensive. I could precompute the unfolded indices and gather, but that's still memory-bound. Let me stick with the custom direct kernel and see. Actually, one more idea: use vectorized computation. In the kernel, instead of each thread computing 1 output, what if each thread computes 2 contiguous spatial positions (same channel)? This would double the arithmetic per thread and allow better instruction-level parallelism. Let me try: each thread computes 2 outputs (ow and ow+16 maybe, or just 2 adjacent ow within the same chunk?) If each thread computes 2 outputs, I need 16 threads per channel in a warp instead of 32. So 16 threads x 16 channels = 256 threads total. But channels are 96, so 96/16=6 channel groups. Hmm, this is getting complicated. Let me first try the simpler version, measure it, and then optimize. Let me write the complete Python module now.
